18:630:53467:22:22:70:524:96:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Liberalization”  Phase  with  the  same  distance  dummies  (βTd1,βTd2,βTd3).   The  specification  in
18:631:53569:22:0:70:524:74:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:column (1) includes year and municipality fixed effects and the Bartik index of employment as
19:632:53664:0:22:70:524:774:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:controls.  It includes only border-region municipalities in the analysis.  In column (2) we add
19:633:53759:22:22:70:524:752:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a set of seven region-specific trends (NUTS II definition of region).  In column (3) we perform
19:634:53854:22:22:70:524:730:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the estimates including also municipalities in the non-border region as additional control group.
19:635:53951:22:22:70:524:708:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:The  standard  errors  of  the  estimates  are  clustered  at  the  commuting  zone  level  (a  group  of
19:636:54056:22:22:70:524:686:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:municipalities that constitutes a labor market) as unobserved variables can be correlated at the
19:637:54152:22:22:70:524:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:local level.  Let us notice that the estimated coefficients are quite similar across specifications
19:638:54251:22:22:70:524:642:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(1)-(3), with the specification in column (2) being the one we usually adopt in other parts of the
19:639:54349:22:22:70:524:620:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:analysis.  Three findings emerge clearly from these estimates.  First, for the Transition period
19:640:54445:22:22:70:524:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the coefficients are very small (smaller than one percentage point) and rarely significant in each
19:641:54543:22:21:70:524:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:distance interval.  While they are prevalently positive, they are always smaller than 1.2 log points
19:642:54643:21:22:70:524:555:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and they do not show statistical significance in the first two distance bins (0−10 and 10−20
19:643:54735:22:22:70:524:533:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:minutes from the border).  Second, in the Full Liberalization phase the coefficients are positive,
19:644:54833:22:22:70:524:511:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:significant and between three and four percentage points for the first two bins.  In the third bin
19:645:54931:22:22:70:524:489:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(20−30 km) the coefficient is around 1.3 to 1.6 percentage points in specifications (2) and (3),
19:646:55027:22:22:70:524:467:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:denoting a small increase in CBW relative to the pre-1999 phase.  This group can be seen as
19:647:55118:22:22:70:524:445:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:“weakly” treated.  Finally, the inclusion or not of the NBR municipalities as controls, which is
19:648:55214:22:22:70:524:423:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:done in specification (3), does not make any difference (vis-`a-vis specification 2).  Considering
19:649:55312:22:22:70:524:401:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:municipalities in the BR over 30 minutes distance from the border as control is just as good as
19:650:55407:22:22:70:524:379:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:using also those in the NBR. Overall full liberalization seems to have triggered an increase in
19:651:55502:22:22:70:524:357:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the supply of CBW by three to four percent of the employment, only in municipalities 0−20
19:652:55591:22:22:70:190:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:minutes from the border.
19:653:55615:22:22:87:524:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:In the estimates of columns (1)-(3) we assume that municipalities at 30 or more km from
19:654:55702:22:21:70:524:291:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  border  are  a  good  counter-factual  for  municipalities  near  the  border,  conditional  on  the
19:655:55807:21:22:70:524:270:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:control variables.  In column (4) we adopt a different strategy to choose the counter-factual, and
19:656:55905:22:22:70:524:248:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we focus only on the municipalities closest to the border, which should be the most intensely
19:657:55998:22:22:70:524:226:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:treated.   In  it  we  identify  the  coefficients  on  the  interaction  of  the  policy  variables  and  the
19:658:56108:22:22:70:524:204:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:0−10 minute distance dummy using matched pairs of “treated” and “control” municipalities.
19:659:56197:22:22:70:524:182:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Specifically, we match each municipality in the 0−10 minute time range from the border with
19:660:56288:22:22:70:524:160:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:another municipality in one of two control groups, the area in the border region at 30 minutes
19:661:56382:22:22:70:524:138:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:or more from the border or in the non-border region.  We pool the 3 waves from the pre-reform
19:662:56475:22:22:70:524:116:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:period (1994-1998) to gain precision and match municipalities based on the variance-weighted
19:663:56567:22:22:70:524:94:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:difference in the employment share of three education and four age groups, the share of foreign
19:664:56662:22:0:70:524:72:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:workers, the share of twelve broad industry groups, the mean log wage level, and the employment
20:665:56759:0:22:70:524:774:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:level and density (number of workers per land area).  With this approach we are able to match
20:666:56852:22:22:70:523:752:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:327 municipalities in the 0−10 minute area of the border region to 243 control municipalities.
20:667:56946:22:22:87:524:730:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We then include only the group of matched municipalities in a regression similar to 2 with
20:668:57036:22:22:70:524:708:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:only the interactions of the policy phases with the 0−10 dummies.  We additionally include as
20:669:57129:22:22:70:524:686:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:many matched-pair dummies as there are municipalities in the 0-10 interval.  In this case, the
20:670:57223:22:22:70:524:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:coefficient on the interaction betweenFREEtandTransitiontand the dummyI(distm<10)
20:671:57304:22:22:70:524:642:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is  estimated  based  only  on  the  difference  between  the  treated  municipality  and  its  matched
20:672:57407:22:22:70:524:620:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:control, allowing for any common unobservable factor in the two matched areas before and after
20:673:57501:22:22:70:524:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the border opening.  Column (4) shows the coefficients of this type of estimate.  This method
20:674:57594:22:21:70:524:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:produces  a  precisely  estimated  zero  value  (0.1  percentage  points)  for  the  Transitional  phase
20:675:57698:21:22:70:524:555:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and  a  value  close  to  four  percentage  points  for  the  Full  Liberalization  phase.   The  change  in
20:676:57806:22:22:70:524:533:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:CBW between the 0−10 minute municipalities and their matched control is even more clearly
20:677:57895:22:22:70:435:511:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:concentrated in the Full Liberalization period, and is sizable and significant.
20:678:57974:22:22:87:524:489:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:These results indicate that, considering two municipalities as similar as possible on several
20:679:58067:22:22:70:524:467:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:dimensions prior to 1998, and allowing for any common unobserved trend over time, the munici-
20:680:58160:22:22:70:524:445:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:palities in the 0-10 minute time bin experienced a significant increase in CBW after 2004 relative
20:681:58258:22:22:70:524:423:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to the ones at 30+ minutes.  These results also indicate that the effect arises most clearly in the
20:682:58357:22:22:70:524:401:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Full Liberalization phase, rather than during the Transition.  We will, however, describe the full
20:683:58455:22:22:70:384:379:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:dynamics of the effect when performing the year-by-year analysis.
20:684:58520:22:22:87:524:357:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Column (5)-(7) show the change in CBW, by education group, as percent of total employment
20:685:58609:22:22:70:523:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in 1998.  The estimates show that, especially for the 0−10 and 10−20 bins, highly- and middle-
20:686:58703:22:22:70:524:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:educated CBW were the groups whose supply increased more significantly, already starting in the
20:687:58798:22:21:70:524:291:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Transition and much more in the Full Liberalization phase.  Low-skilled CBW actually declined
20:688:58891:21:22:70:524:270:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:somewhat in the 0−10 minute region in both periods.  This implies that the net increase in CBW
20:689:58985:22:22:70:524:248:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:was also a net increase in relative supply of highly skilled workers for the municipalities close
20:690:59082:22:22:70:524:226:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to the border.24The municipality at 0−10 minutes from the border experienced a growth of
20:691:59170:22:22:70:524:204:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:middle and high educated CBW by 5 percent of total employment and a decline in less educated
20:692:59262:22:22:70:524:182:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:CBW  by  1.5  percent  of  total  employment.   Not  only  did  more  workers  become  available  to
20:693:59362:22:22:70:523:160:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:municipalities in those areas, but they were significantly more highly educated than natives.25
20:694:59457:22:22:70:524:138:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Finally Column (8) of Table 2 shows the estimated coefficient if we consider the change of all
20:695:59551:22:16:70:524:116:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:foreign born, summing CBW and immigrants (I)in municipalitymand yeart, standardized by

22:748:64385:22:22:70:524:467:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:It is hard to say whether the anticipation or the initial measures started having an effect already

22:761:65535:22:22:87:524:182:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The estimates reported in Panel D provide additional information and confirm two important
22:762:65625:22:22:70:524:160:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:characteristics of the CBW increase after 1999.  First, we see that the post-1999 growth of CBW
22:763:65720:22:22:70:524:138:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in the 0−10 distance range (the only coefficient reported) is due to the increase of middle and
22:764:65815:22:16:70:524:116:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:highly educated CBW (dark and medium dashed lines).  Their pre-1999 trend was very flat and
22:765:65906:16:15:79:524:100:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:27When compared to 1998, the point estimates of 2002 and 2004 for the 0−10 bin seem to reveal a positive
22:766:66010:15:11:70:524:85:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:deviation.   However,  if  one  compares  them  with  an  average  pre-1999  (1994,  1996  and  1998)  there  is  really  no
22:767:66134:11:0:70:200:74:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:deviation (as shown in Table 2).
23:768:66168:0:22:70:524:774:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:their  growth  took  off,  significantly,  only  after  2004.   Second,  the  growth  of  low-skilled  CBW
23:769:66274:22:22:70:524:752:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:remains close to zero (light dashed line) over the whole period, with possibly even a decrease
23:770:66368:22:22:70:524:730:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:relative to the pre-1999 period.  The noise of the data for low-skilled CBW, and the fact that
23:771:66462:22:22:70:524:708:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:their coefficient remained rather flat for most of the period, suggests that this group remained at
23:772:66561:22:22:70:524:686:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:pre-1999 level of mobility.  The Transitional phase and the Full Liberalization of CBW therefore
23:773:66657:22:22:70:514:664:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:implied a clear increase in supply in medium- and skilled CBW but not in less-skilled ones.28
23:774:66750:22:22:87:524:642:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:An additional feature of the CBW changes reported in Figure 3 is worth emphasizing.  The
23:775:66838:22:22:70:524:620:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:supply of CBW in municipalities 0−10 minutes from the border increased progressively starting
23:776:66931:22:22:70:524:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in 2004.  Clearly there was no immediate “jump”produced by free CBW movement, but rather
23:777:67019:22:21:70:524:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a progressive growth in their share of employment from 2004 to 2010.  Likely, the opening of the
23:778:67115:21:22:70:524:555:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:border made firms recognize opportunities to hire new highly skilled CBW and it encouraged
23:779:67205:22:22:70:524:533:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:them to create more jobs, slowly affecting their employment.  This emphasizes the importance
23:780:67297:22:22:70:524:511:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of analyzing the “total” rather than “partial” effects of immigration over time, which accounts
23:781:67392:22:22:70:524:489:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for several margins of adjustment.  The dynamics of capital investment,  the transition of na-
23:782:67486:22:22:70:524:467:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:tives  across  occupations,  firms’  growth  and  birth  of  new  firms  occurredat  the  same  timeas
23:783:67588:22:22:70:524:445:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the CBW inflow took place, and in anticipation and response to that inflow.  Hence the com-
23:784:67679:22:22:70:524:423:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:parison of outcomes with the changes in CBW across years produces a picture of simultaneous
23:785:67770:22:22:70:524:401:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:adjustments  in  response  to  a  cumulated  increase  of  immigrants.   What  is  new  in  this  paper
23:786:67873:22:22:70:524:379:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is  that  we  can  analyze  first  the  effect  of  the  immigration  policy  on  immigration  flows  before
23:787:67981:22:22:70:524:357:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:analyzing the consequences of immigrants on natives.  Our study, and the work of Ruffner and
23:788:68073:22:22:70:524:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Siegenthaler  (2016),  are  among  the  very  few  (as  far  as  we  know)  that  track  the  effects  of  a
23:789:68181:22:22:70:524:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:permanent immigration policy change in a developed country.  Our focus is on the analysis of its
23:790:68277:22:21:70:524:291:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:consequences both on immigrants and on labor market outcomes and on the internal migration
23:791:68367:21:22:70:524:270:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and inter-sectoral and inter-job dynamic adjustment of native workers.  The work of Ruffner and
23:792:68462:22:22:70:524:248:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Siegenthaler (2016), complementary to this, analyzes the impact on firm creation, innovation,
23:793:68555:22:16:70:189:226:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:R&D and productivity.29
23:794:68578:16:15:79:524:210:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:28In Appendix Table A2, we report the change in the number of CBW (relative to total employment in 1998)
23:795:68682:15:11:70:524:195:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:for 24 separate occupation categories in the SESS data.  This shows that the largest contribution to the overall
23:796:68794:11:11:70:524:184:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:increase in CBW came from middle and highly-paying occupations, particularly from occupations entrusted with
23:797:68902:11:7:70:444:173:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:reviewing/consulting/certifying, R&D, analyze/program/operating and medical occupations.
23:798:68990:7:15:79:524:166:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:29The paper Dustmann, Sch ̈onberg and Stuhler (2017) instead considers a temporary change of cross-border
23:799:69095:15:11:70:524:151:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:workers in Germany (reversed after two years) leading to inflow of mainly unskilled workers in a period (1991-1993)
23:800:69210:11:0:70:313:140:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:of deep crisis in Eastern European institutions and markets.
24:801:69272:0:34:70:395:774:14:g_d0_f3:14:g_d0_f3:C:5    Effects on Native Labor Market Outcomes
24:802:69316:34:29:70:244:740:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:5.1    Effects on Native Wages
24:803:69346:29:22:70:524:711:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:The  findings  in  the  previous  section  establish  that  by  2010  the  policy  of  free  labor  market
24:804:69452:22:21:70:524:689:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:access for CBW increased their supply by about four to five percentage points of employment in
24:805:69546:21:22:70:524:668:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:municipalities within a 0−10 minute commute from the border.  It also increased their supply
24:806:69638:22:22:70:524:646:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:by a similar amount in municipalities 10−20 minutes from the border.  Beyond that commuting
24:807:69729:22:22:70:524:624:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:time, the effects on CBW supply are one percent or smaller.  Tertiary and secondary-educated
24:808:69821:22:22:70:524:602:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:CBW were the groups responsible for the whole increase.  Did this result in depressed wages
24:809:69912:22:22:70:524:580:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:or employment for highly-skilled natives?  Were the wages of other groups of workers affected
24:810:70005:22:22:70:524:558:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:through complementarity?  Or did firms respond by creating jobs for highly-educated and absorb
24:811:70099:22:22:70:524:536:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the larger supply of CBW? And did native workers respond by moving from close to the border
24:812:70190:22:22:70:452:514:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to other areas?  Or by changing jobs?  And which industries were more affected?
24:813:70269:22:22:87:524:492:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:To answer these questions, we first analyze the effect of the policy changes on wages, em-
24:814:70359:22:22:70:524:470:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ployment, and hours worked by Swiss natives in aggregate.  We estimate a regression in the form
24:815:70454:22:22:70:524:448:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of equation (2) with municipalities as units and the average logarithm of native hourly wages as
24:816:70550:22:22:70:524:426:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the dependent variable.30We show the main estimated coefficients interacting the policy phase
24:817:70643:22:21:70:524:404:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and the distance bins in Column (1) and (2) of Table 3a.  If one thinks of the change in supply
24:818:70738:21:22:70:524:383:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of CBW as the dependent variable and of the policy dummies interacted with distance bins as
24:819:70829:22:22:70:524:361:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the instrument, then the coefficient reported are those from a reduced-form IV regression.  The
24:820:70924:22:22:70:524:339:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:coefficient estimates represent the intention-to-treat effect (ITT) and the treated groups are the
24:821:71022:22:22:70:524:317:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:0−10 and 10−20 minute bins.  The point estimates of the impact on native wages can then
24:822:71109:22:22:70:524:295:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:be used to derive the elasticity of their response to the change in the supply of CBW, estimated
24:823:71205:22:22:70:524:273:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in Table 2, dividing the first coefficient by the second.  Column (1) shows estimates from the
24:824:71299:22:22:70:524:251:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:specifications with all controls and including only municipalities in the Border Region.  Column
24:825:71395:22:22:70:524:229:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(2) shows the estimates when NBR are included in the control group.31Table 3b, then shows
24:826:71484:22:16:70:524:207:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the coefficients estimated separately by education group.  Column (1) in Panel A in Table 3b
24:827:71576:16:15:79:524:191:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:30Table A3 in the Appendix shows the estimates using log of monthly wages as dependent variable.  Monthly

25:845:72330:0:22:70:524:774:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:uses average log hourly wage of highly-educated natives as the outcome.  Column (1) of Panel B
25:846:72424:22:22:70:523:752:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:and Panel C does the same for middle educated and low educated native workers, respectively.32
25:847:72518:22:22:70:524:730:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:The point estimates of Table 3b show the “total” effect of immigrants on the wage of each ed-
25:848:72611:22:22:70:524:708:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ucation group of natives.  Those of table 3a show the total effect on average native wages.  The
25:849:72707:22:22:70:524:686:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:coefficients captures the impact on native wages both from competition of CBW with similar
25:850:72797:22:22:70:386:664:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:skills and from complementarity from CBW with different skills.33
25:851:72862:22:22:87:524:642:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Inspecting the estimates of Table 3a, the first consideration is that the effects on the average
25:852:72958:22:22:70:524:620:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wages  in  the  0−10  and  10−20  minute  bins  are  very  small  and  never  significant  both  in
25:853:73057:22:22:70:524:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  “Transition”  and  in  the  “Full  Liberalization”  phase.   The  point  estimates  for  the  0−10
25:854:73160:22:21:70:524:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and  10−20  bins  are  usually  positive  but  small  (never  larger  than  1.8  log  points)  and  never
25:855:73265:21:22:70:524:555:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:significant at the 5% confidence level.  Since we found that the post-2004 liberalization increased
25:856:73364:22:22:70:524:533:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the share of CBW by about four to five percent of the employment in bins at 0−20 minute
25:857:73451:22:22:70:524:511:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:distance,  the implied elasticity for the average wage effect is compatible with values between
25:858:73546:22:22:70:524:489:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:-0.3 and 0.3, using the estimates of the 0−10 and 10−20 interacted with Full-liberalization.
25:859:73638:22:22:70:524:467:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Given  the  standard  errors,  a  zero  effect  cannot  be  rejected  at  any  level  of  confidence.   An
25:860:73744:22:22:70:524:445:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:explanation based on a classical model of “change in supply for fixed demand” does not work
25:861:73835:22:22:70:524:423:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:well  in  this  case  (as  there  is  no  evidence  in  the  short  run  of  a  negative  effect).   This  is  even
25:862:73950:22:22:70:524:401:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:clearer  when  we  look  at  the  impact  by  education  group  shown  in  Table  3b.   As  documented
25:863:74052:22:22:70:524:379:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:above,  Full  Liberalization  produced  a  larger  inflow  of  highly  educated  CBW  relative  to  less-
25:864:74157:22:22:70:524:357:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:educated ones.  Hence the canonical “partial effects” model would imply a negative wage effect
25:865:74251:22:22:70:524:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on highly-educated natives and positive effects on low-educated ones.  To the contrary, the most
25:866:74347:22:22:70:524:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:significant effects, which are robust across specifications, are reported in column (1) of Panel A
25:867:74445:22:21:70:524:291:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of table 3b and show apositivepositive coefficient on the wages of the highly-educated in the
25:868:74538:21:22:70:524:270:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Full Liberalization phase, in the order of + 4% in the 0−10 and the 10−20 minute bins.  The
25:869:74629:22:22:70:524:248:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:coefficient is positive but not significant (equal to 1.6%) for the 20−30 minute bin.  Smaller
25:870:74723:22:22:70:524:226:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:positive  effects,  especially  in  the  0−10  minute  bins  appear  already  in  the  Transition  phase.
25:871:74828:22:22:70:524:204:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:At the same time, no wage effects are significant at the 5%-level for the middle educated and
25:872:74921:22:22:70:524:182:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for the low educated (Panel B and C, Column (1)).  Some point estimates are estimated to be
25:873:75012:22:16:70:524:160:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:negative in any of the phases.  The changes in wages of highly-educated natives are consistent
25:874:75106:16:15:79:524:144:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:32The random sampling of the SESS implies that some municipalities have missing information for the dependent
25:875:75215:15:11:70:524:129:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:variable of some education groups.  This explains the difference in the number of observations across education
25:876:75326:11:11:70:524:118:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:groups (panels) of a particular specification in the Table 3b and also in Table 3a.  We found similar results when
25:877:75440:11:11:70:524:107:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:dropping municipalities below the 20th percentile of aggregate employment each year or dropping municipalities
25:878:75550:11:11:70:524:96:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:that had missing information in aggregate employment in any year.  These results are shown for log hourly wages
25:879:75661:11:7:70:426:85:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:and total hours as dependent variables in the Appendix Tables A4 and A5, respectively.
25:880:75747:7:15:79:524:78:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:33See Ottaviano and Peri (2012) for a more formal argument about the estimation of a total effect of immigrants
25:881:75858:15:0:70:482:63:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:aggregating all the direct competition and indirect complementarity effects from different skill groups.
26:882:75964:0:22:70:524:774:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:with  a  causal  effect  of  the  increase  in  CBW,  but  not  with  a  simple  neoclassical  model.   The
26:883:76071:22:22:70:524:752:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:channels of this complementarity/productivity effect can be several.  We suggest three of them
26:884:76165:22:22:70:524:730:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:here that have been recently proposed in relation to immigrants.  First, a larger supply of CBW
26:885:76260:22:22:70:524:708:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:pushes firms to expand and create new jobs (as in a search model `a la Chassamboulli and Palivos
26:886:76356:22:22:70:524:686:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(2014)) with some of those jobs filled by natives.  Second, firms’ return to investing in equipment
26:887:76455:22:22:70:524:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:capital  and  new  technologies  that  complement  the  skills  of  CBW  increases,  and  investment
26:888:76555:22:22:70:524:642:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:enhances productivity of native workers too (as in Lewis, 2011, Lafortune, Tessada and Lewis
26:889:76647:22:22:70:524:620:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(2015) or Peri (2012)).  Finally, local human capital externalities of the type analyzed in Moretti
26:890:76746:22:22:70:524:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(2004a,b; 2010), and occupational upgrading of native highly skilled jobs as described in Peri and
26:891:76844:22:21:70:524:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Sparber (2009), could increase productivity through learning, higher efficiency and technological
26:892:76941:21:22:70:524:555:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:adoption.  Such models can generate increases in productivity for groups whose supply increases.
26:893:77037:22:22:70:524:533:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Increased productivity and job creation for high skilled natives are also consistent with the firm-
26:894:77136:22:22:70:524:511:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:level evidence presented by Ruffner and Siegenthaler (2016) and reviewed in Section 6.5 below.

26:897:77417:22:22:70:524:445:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:only and the 95% confidence interval obtained from specification (3) with the average logarithm

26:899:77605:22:22:70:524:401:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in Panel B, and the same wage measure for middle- and low-educated natives in Panels C and

26:901:77784:22:22:87:524:357:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:There is some variability in the estimates before 1999, but there is no evidence of a systematic
26:902:77880:22:22:70:524:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:pre-trend.  None of Panels A-D, show evidence that the intensely treated municipalities (0−10
26:903:77973:22:22:70:524:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:bin) had a systematically different wage trends relative to the control group before 1999.  This
26:904:78069:22:21:70:524:291:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is consistent with our identifying assumptions.  For the aggregate log wages we observe a small
26:905:78164:21:22:70:524:270:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and non-significant drop in 2004 (the point estimate is half the size of the standard error) and
26:906:78260:22:22:70:524:248:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:no change after then.  This amounts to no evidence of an average wage effect.  Panel B, relative
26:907:78356:22:22:70:524:226:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to highly skilled wages, shows instead positive and significant coefficients after 2004, reaching
26:908:78453:22:22:70:524:204:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:about  four  to  five  percent  by  2010.   The  timing  and  scale  of  the  effect  is  consistent  with  a
26:909:78562:22:22:70:524:182:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:positive effect of CBW on productivity of high skilled Swiss workers with a semi-elasticity of
26:910:78656:22:22:70:524:160:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:about one (namely an increase of CBW of one percent of employment produces a one percent
26:911:78744:22:22:70:524:138:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage increase).  The other two groups of native workers (middle- and less-educated) have smaller
26:912:78840:22:16:70:524:116:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and non significant wage changes, with the low-educated experiencing a drop between 2000 and
26:913:78932:16:15:79:524:100:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:34In  the  reported  figures  we  estimated  the  equation  (3)  only  for  municipalities  in  the  border  region  and  we
26:914:79056:15:11:70:524:85:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:always include a full set of municipality and year fixed effects,  region-specific trends and an education-specific
26:915:79171:11:0:70:164:74:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:Bartik index for wages.
27:916:79196:0:22:70:524:774:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:2004 and a more mixed path between 2004 and 2010, while the middle educated have a small
27:917:79284:22:22:70:524:752:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:drop in 2004 as well.  Yet this change is also not significant statistically.35Combining evidence
27:918:79381:22:22:70:524:730:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:from Tables 3a, 3b and Figure 4, we find consistent evidence of a significant wage increase for
27:919:79476:22:22:70:524:708:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:highly-educated natives in the areas where and periods when CBW increased as a consequence
27:920:79566:22:22:70:524:686:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of  the  new  policies.   Both  middle-skill  native  workers  and  native  workers  as  a  whole  did  not
27:921:79673:22:22:70:524:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:experience  any  significant  effect.   Less-skilled  workers  possibly  experienced  a  small,  negative,
27:922:79779:22:38:70:191:642:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:still not significant effect.
27:923:79808:38:29:70:406:604:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:5.2    Effects on Natives’ Employment and Hours Worked
27:924:79862:29:22:70:524:575:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:A second important aspect of the potential labor market effects of CBW is the impact on native
27:925:79956:22:21:70:524:553:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:labor supply.  While positively affecting productivity of high skilled natives, did CBW displaced
27:926:80053:21:22:70:524:532:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:labor supply of native workers?  We measure this variable as the logarithm of total number of
27:927:80146:22:22:70:524:510:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:native workers or,  alternatively,  as the logarithm of total hours worked by natives.  The first
27:928:80243:22:22:70:524:488:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:measure  captures  only  the  extensive  margin  of  labor  supply,  while  the  second  combines  the
27:929:80345:22:22:70:524:466:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:extensive and intensive margin.  Table 3a, columns (3) and (4) shows the coefficients of interest
27:930:80442:22:22:70:524:444:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for total native employment and columns (5) and (6) for total native work hours, respectively.
27:931:80536:22:22:70:524:422:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Table 3b, panel A, Columns (2) and (3) shows the same effects when considering only highly-
27:932:80627:22:22:70:524:400:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:educated natives, Panel B when looking at middle-educated natives and Panel C for low-educated
27:933:80721:22:22:70:524:378:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:only.  Figure 5 plots the coefficients estimates from regressions as specified in equation (3) for
27:934:80819:22:22:70:524:356:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:time bin 0−10 minutes, with total hours worked by all natives as the dependent variable in Panel
27:935:80915:22:22:70:524:334:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:A and by education group (Panel B-D), respectively.36The estimates for employment and hours
27:936:81006:22:22:70:524:312:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:worked are somewhat noisy and the standard errors can be as large as 6 percent and sometimes
27:937:81098:22:22:70:524:290:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:they exceed 15 percent for the small group of less-educated.  They, however, never show evidence
27:938:81194:22:21:70:524:268:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of an “crowding out” effect on native employment or native hours worked (which would imply
27:939:81284:21:22:70:524:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:negative and significant coefficients).  The point estimates for the aggregate employment and
27:940:81377:22:22:70:524:225:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:hours effects during the Full Liberalization period are not significant and are usually positive (see
27:941:81478:22:22:70:524:203:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:also Figure 5, Panel A). For individual education groups, we see some positive and significant
27:942:81572:22:22:70:524:181:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:coefficients on the highly-educated employment and working hours mainly in the 20−30 bin,
27:943:81661:22:16:70:524:159:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:which was only weakly treated.  This may imply some spillover effects from the bins closer to
27:944:81754:16:15:79:524:143:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:35We find qualitatively similar results when we use two alternative definitions of workers’ skills and when we
27:945:81864:15:11:70:524:128:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:use two distance bins (0−15 and 15−30 minutes) only.  Figure A3 shows the year effects for 15-minute distance
27:946:81973:11:11:70:524:117:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:partitions (Panel I) and for wage tercile and occupation based partitions (Panel II and III). In all cases, the effect
27:947:82091:11:11:70:524:106:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:on highly skilled wage is positive and usually significant for the region closest to the border in the post-2004 years.
27:948:82210:11:7:70:431:95:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:For the other two education groups, the effects are usually close to zero and insignificant.
27:949:82302:7:15:79:524:88:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:36The regressions used to obtain the coefficients of Figure 5 includes year and municipality fixed effects, region-
27:950:82417:15:0:70:489:73:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:specific trends and education group specific Bartik index for log total hours as specified in equation (2).
28:951:82526:0:22:70:524:774:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the border into the 20−30 bin, which we will explore in the next section.  For less-educated,
28:952:82619:22:22:70:524:752:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:there seems to be a positive but not significant (noisy) effect near the border for employment
28:953:82713:22:22:70:524:730:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and hours worked.  The increase in employment and hours worked by less-educated natives can
28:954:82804:22:22:70:524:708:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:suggest an increase in the demand for their services resulting from the complementarity with
28:955:82896:22:22:70:524:686:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  services  of  skilled  CBW.  This  demand  could  have  triggered  a  rather  elastic  labor  supply
28:956:83001:22:22:70:524:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:response of less-skilled workers without affecting their wages much.  The estimates, however, are
28:957:83098:22:22:70:482:642:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:not significant at standard confidence levels and so we should take them with caution.
28:958:83184:22:22:87:524:620:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Altogether, the estimated wage and employment coefficients are consistent with a positive
28:959:83273:22:22:70:524:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:complementary/productivity effect of CBW on high skilled native workers. Moreover they do not
28:960:83366:22:21:70:524:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:suggest crowding out, or pure competition from CBW. In particular, the positive and significant
28:961:83461:21:22:70:524:555:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:effect on the wages of highly-educated and the null to positive overall effects on their employment
28:962:83560:22:22:70:524:533:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:are at odds with pure competition effects that would prevail in a canonical neoclassical model.
28:963:83655:22:22:70:524:511:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:In order to understand better the channels of response of native workers and firms that allow
28:964:83748:22:22:70:524:489:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:such a positive impact on native workers, we analyze in greater detail some specific transitions
28:965:83844:22:38:70:431:467:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of native workers into and out of employment, and between jobs or regions.
28:966:83918:38:29:70:213:429:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:5.3    Robustness Checks
28:967:83942:29:22:70:524:400:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:To further alleviate the concern that unobserved variables may create a bias in the estimates
28:968:84035:22:22:70:524:378:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the effect of CBW in the wage and employment regressions, we perform several robustness
28:969:84125:22:22:70:524:356:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:checks, reported in Appendix Tables A6a for aggregate variables and A6b, panel A-C, for skill-
28:970:84219:22:22:70:524:334:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:specific groups.  As a first check, shown in that Table in columns (1) and (5), we estimate our
28:971:84314:22:22:70:524:312:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:baseline specification (2), using less conservative standard errors, clustered at the municipality
28:972:84412:22:22:70:524:290:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:level instead of the commuting zone level.  Compared to the baseline estimates above (in columns
28:973:84508:22:21:70:524:268:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(1) and (5) of Tables 3a and 3b), however, this check leads to almost no change in the significance
28:974:84607:21:22:70:524:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:level of the coefficients.  Second, while we found no evidence for a pre-1999 trend and our baseline
28:975:84707:22:22:70:524:225:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:specification already includes separate trends for the seven NUTS II regions, columns (2) and (6)
28:976:84804:22:22:70:524:203:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:allow for a separate trend for each of the 26 Cantons in Switzerland.  Adding the canton-specific
28:977:84901:22:22:70:523:181:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:trends we observe a small decrease in the positive 0−10 and 10−20 wage coefficient on high
28:978:84991:22:22:70:524:159:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:skilled during the Full Liberalization period and a small increases in the 10−20 and 20−30
28:979:85081:22:22:70:524:137:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employment and hours coefficient.  However, the differences are minor.  A third robustness check
28:980:85177:22:22:70:524:115:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:has to do with the concern that the economies of large cities near the border, such as Geneva,
28:981:85271:22:22:70:524:93:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Basel and Lugano, may drive the whole ”attraction effect” for CBW after Full Liberalization.
28:982:85363:22:0:70:524:71:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Hence, we estimate a specification excluding border cities in Columns (3) and (7) of Appendix
29:983:85458:0:22:70:524:774:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Table A6a and A6b.  This change does not have any significant impact on the results.  Finally,
29:984:85552:22:22:70:524:752:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to test whether the main results in the municipalities closest to the border are robust to the
29:985:85646:22:22:70:524:730:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:choice of an alternative control group, we use the matching method, for the municipalities in
29:986:85739:22:22:70:524:708:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the 0−10 minute region,  and we match them with most similar ones in the +30min region.
29:987:85826:22:22:70:524:686:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:The  estimated  coefficients  are  significantly  positive  for  the  high  skilled  wage  effect  after  full
29:988:85936:22:22:70:524:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:liberalization, confirming that the positive productivity effect of CBW on that group seems the
29:989:86031:22:45:70:329:642:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:strongest result of our wage and employment analysis.
29:990:86084:45:34:70:504:597:14:g_d0_f3:14:g_d0_f3:C:6    Native Workers’ Transitions and Adjustment Mechanisms
29:991:86142:34:29:70:316:563:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:6.1    Spillovers Due to Internal Migration
29:992:86185:29:22:70:524:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:As municipalities are small open economies, part of the effect of a larger supply of CBW may
29:993:86277:22:21:70:524:512:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:spill  over  to  other  municipalities  in  different  ways.   Of  particular  interest  is  how  inflows  of
29:994:86386:21:22:70:524:491:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:CBW may affect the ways Swiss workers move to other municipalities nearby or in the rest of
29:995:86477:22:22:70:524:469:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Switzerland, and how they may affect the inflow from other Swiss municipalities.  A significant
29:996:86572:22:22:70:524:447:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:movement of Swiss workers to other municipalities, possibly within the border region but farther
29:997:86668:22:22:70:524:425:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:from  the  border,  may  affect  the  municipalities  farther  than  30  minutes  from  the  border  and
29:998:86772:22:22:70:524:403:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:hence question our assumption that they are a valid control group.  Past research (e.g.  Card
29:999:86865:22:22:70:523:381:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and DiNardo, 2000; Peri and Sparber, 2011a) has not found significant outflows of natives in
29:1000:86957:22:22:70:524:359:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:response to immigration.  However, recent papers (e.g.  Dustmann, Sch ̈onberg and Stuhler, 2017)
29:1001:87053:22:22:70:524:337:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:have suggested that inflows of natives from other areas may be reduced following immigration,
29:1002:87146:22:22:70:490:315:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:producing a “crowding out” of potential natives who would have moved into the region.
29:1003:87231:22:22:87:524:293:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:In table 4 we show the key findings about the changes in net flows (column (1)) and inflows
29:1004:87322:22:22:70:524:271:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(columns 2 and 3) and outflows (columns 4 and 5) from/to other Swiss municipalities in the Full
29:1005:87417:22:22:70:524:249:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Liberalization period.  Additionally we divide these flows between municipalities within the 30
29:1006:87512:22:21:70:524:227:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:minute range from the border (columns 2 and 4) and those farther than 30 minutes (columns 3
29:1007:87603:21:22:70:524:206:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and 5).  For compactness, we only report the coefficients in the Full liberalization period, first
29:1008:87701:22:22:70:524:184:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:considering all workers (panel A) and then the three education groups separately (Panels B-D).
29:1009:87795:22:22:70:524:162:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Results in the Transitional period are weaker and reported in Appendix Table A7.  In Table 4
29:1010:87887:22:22:70:524:140:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the dependent variable for each column is a different flow into or out of a municipality during
29:1011:87982:22:22:70:524:118:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a year, standardized by the initial employment of the municipalitym.  Hence the dependent
29:1012:88071:22:22:70:331:96:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:variable is a migration/rate as percent of employment.
29:1013:88125:22:0:87:524:74:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:To construct these variables, we use the Swiss Labor Force Survey (SLFS) as a complemen-
30:1014:88215:0:22:70:524:774:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:tary data set.  Most individuals in the SLFS were interviewed for two consecutive years,  and
30:1015:88308:22:22:70:524:752:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we can calculate the flow variables at the municipal level using the employment status and the
30:1016:88402:22:22:70:454:730:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:place of work for each individual in the previous or the next year, respectively.37
30:1017:88485:22:22:87:524:708:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Panel  A  of  Table  4  shows  that  the  coefficients  on  net  flows  (column  1),  inflows  (columns
30:1018:88588:22:22:70:524:686:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(2) and (3)) and outflows (columns (4) and (5)) for all Swiss workers are very small and never
30:1019:88682:22:22:70:524:664:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:significant.  This implies that there is no sign of spillovers effect or indirect impact on potential
30:1020:88783:22:22:70:524:642:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:movers during Full Liberalization.  When looking at the highly educated, which is the only group
30:1021:88879:22:22:70:524:620:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for which we find a robust wage effect, in panel B of Table 4, we see no significant coefficient
30:1022:88975:22:22:70:524:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for the net migration nor for gross inflows or outflows from/to the area within 30 minutes from
30:1023:89070:22:21:70:524:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the border (columns (2) and (4)).  There is one coefficient which is significant for the inflow and
30:1024:89169:21:22:70:524:555:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:outflow of workers from/to regions at more than 30 minutes from the border.  These are for the
30:1025:89263:22:22:70:524:533:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:10−20 minute bin, and they have the same sign and similar magnitude, implying smaller inflows
30:1026:89356:22:22:70:524:511:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and smaller outflows of similar amounts and so, in net, no effect.  Hence there is no significant
30:1027:89453:22:22:70:524:489:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:evidence that net flows of highly educated Swiss workers between treated municipalities and any
30:1028:89548:22:22:70:524:467:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the considered distance bins has played a significant role in the adjustment.  In panel C we
30:1029:89643:22:22:70:524:445:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:see no evidence of net flows for middle educated while for less educated there is one positive
30:1030:89737:22:22:70:524:423:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:coefficient  on  net  with  the  municipalities  at  10−20  minutes  from  the  border.   This  may  be
30:1031:89840:22:22:70:524:401:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:somewhat consistent with the positive (albeit only not marginally significant) employment effect
30:1032:89936:22:22:70:524:379:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:showed in Table 3b on employment.  The lack of a clear distance pattern and the imprecision of
30:1033:90030:22:22:70:524:357:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:some of the estimates in panel D, however, do not support too strongly the idea of a significant
30:1034:90126:22:22:70:524:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:internal  migration  response  of  less-educated  workers.   All  estimated  changes  in  net  flows  are
30:1035:90231:22:22:70:524:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:small and are unlikely to have any impact on the average control municipalities (in the regions
30:1036:90326:22:21:70:524:291:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:at more than 30 minutes from the border).  Overall, the inflow of CBW who were mainly highly-
30:1037:90419:21:22:70:524:270:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:educated  increased  their  supply  in  municipalities  very  close  to  the  border  without  creating  a
30:1038:90525:22:22:70:524:248:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:”domino” or ”spillover” effect of native internal migration towards other part of the country.
30:1039:90619:22:22:70:524:226:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We also note here that the flows of Swiss workers from the border region to outside Switzerland
30:1040:90714:22:22:70:524:204:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(as commuters or migrants in the reverse direction from the one considered in the paper) were
30:1041:90807:22:22:70:524:182:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:negligible,  as  the  wage  differential  between  Switzerland  and  municipalities  in  other  countries
30:1042:90912:22:17:70:255:160:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:near the border is large (up to 30%).38
30:1043:90951:17:14:79:524:143:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:37Due to a structural break in this data from year 2010 onward, we only use the years 1996 to 2009 (on a yearly
30:1044:91062:14:11:70:524:129:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:basis) when computing flow variables between two consecutive years (e.g.  in Table 4 or Appendix Table A7).  In
30:1045:91173:11:11:70:524:118:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:contrast, when we exploit the cross-section information as below in section 6.2 (in table 5), we also include the
30:1046:91286:11:11:70:524:107:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:year 2010.  We only use the SLFS to analyze additional mechanisms and worker transitions as the data set is much
30:1047:91398:11:11:70:524:96:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:smaller than the SESS, which we use for our main analysis.  Nevertheless, we replicated all our main results using
30:1048:91512:11:11:70:524:85:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:the SLFS and all results were qualitatively similar.  See Appendix B for more details on the exact calculation of
30:1049:91625:11:7:70:143:74:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:the flow variables.
30:1050:91644:7:0:79:524:67:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:38In fact,  aggregate statistics collected by the Federal Statistical Office suggest that the absolute number of
31:1051:91758:0:29:70:314:774:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:6.2    Moving up the Management Ladder
31:1052:91796:29:22:70:524:745:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Internal geographical mobility was not significantly affected by the increase of CBW. What, then,
31:1053:91893:22:22:70:524:723:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:were  the  mechanisms  put  in  motion to  absorb  the  new  workers  and  to  produce  the  observed
31:1054:91994:22:22:70:524:701:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage/productivity effects on high skilled Swiss workers?  We explore here several alternatives,
31:1055:92089:22:22:70:524:679:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:beginning with the different specialization between CBW and native Swiss workers and the oc-
31:1056:92181:22:22:70:524:657:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:cupational response of natives.  As shown above, highly educated CBW were primarily employed
31:1057:92273:22:22:70:524:635:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in Technical/Scientific and Engineering type of jobs.  One possibility is that, in companies hiring
31:1058:92372:22:22:70:524:613:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:CBW, we may observe an incentive for highly educated Swiss workers to move towards the high
31:1059:92463:22:22:70:524:591:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:end of managerial jobs (see a similar phenomenon in Peri and Sparber (2011b) and Peri, Shih
31:1060:92554:22:22:70:524:569:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and Sparber (2015b)).  Positions in the top organizational tiers of management require knowl-
31:1061:92647:22:21:70:524:547:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:edge of local culture, laws and norms, and possibly a local network of contacts.  Those are more
31:1062:92743:21:22:70:524:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:accessible to a native than to a foreign individual.39The demand for top managerial jobs grew
31:1063:92836:22:22:70:524:504:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:as firms became larger and more productive, as hiring CBW as STEM workers was a way to
31:1064:92922:22:22:70:524:482:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:grow.  Hence we analyze whether natives, especially highly-educated ones, were more likely to
31:1065:93015:22:22:70:524:460:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:become high-level managers in the executive board of Swiss companies after the introduction
31:1066:93106:22:22:70:524:438:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of free mobility of CBW. To do this,  we use a question in the SLFS that identifies if worker
31:1067:93199:22:22:70:524:416:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is in the top executive level (“Direktion/Gesch ̈aftsleitung”) of a firm, which we consider as top
31:1068:93297:22:22:70:188:394:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:management of the firm.
31:1069:93320:22:22:87:524:372:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Table 5 uses as the dependent variable in columns (1) and (2) the share of native workers in
31:1070:93412:22:22:70:524:350:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:executive boards (i.e.  in the top tier of management) relative to native employment.  Column

31:1086:94942:7:0:79:524:60:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:40The latter two effects imply that the change in share of managers must be driven by the entry of individuals
32:1087:95054:0:22:70:524:774:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and hours (column 3 and 4). We infer that for this intermediate group the response is not as clear

32:1112:97403:22:22:70:524:226:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:by immigration.  For instance, Cattaneo, Fiorio and Peri (2015) for countries in the European

33:1132:99235:38:29:70:315:517:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:6.3    Sector-Specific Productivity Growth
33:1133:99277:29:22:70:524:488:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:An  important  dimension  of  the  heterogeneity  of  the  CBW  effect  is  their  different  presence

33:1135:99478:22:22:70:524:444:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:implies a larger direct impact on productivity growth in the sectors that use these skills more
33:1136:99573:22:22:70:524:422:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:intensively.  Those are the high-tech manufacturing sectors and the more knowledge-intensive
33:1137:99665:22:22:70:524:400:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:services (finance, business, human resource management).  If native workers are, at least in the

33:1139:99855:22:22:70:524:356:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and Shimer, 2011) or industry- or firm-specific human capital (Neal, 1995), the inflow of CBW
33:1140:99948:22:22:70:524:334:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:may  have  affected  wages  differentially  across  sectors  and  more  in  those  experiencing  larger
33:1141:100051:22:22:70:106:312:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:inflows.
